</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1220493105">html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}</style>
<p>The <b>pineapple</b><sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> (<i><b>Ananas comosus</b></i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_vegetation" title="Tropical vegetation">tropical plant</a> with an edible fruit; it is the most economically significant plant in the family <a href="/wiki/Bromeliaceae" title="Bromeliaceae">Bromeliaceae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pineapple is indigenous to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, where it has been cultivated for many centuries. The introduction of the pineapple plant to Europe in the <a href="/wiki/17th_century" title="17th century">17th century</a> made it a significant cultural icon of luxury. Since the 1820s, pineapple has been commercially grown in greenhouses and many tropical plantations.
</p><p>Pineapples grow as a small shrub; the individual flowers of the unpollinated plant fuse to form a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_fruit" title="Multiple fruit">multiple fruit</a>. The plant normally propagates from the <a href="/wiki/Offset_(botany)" title="Offset (botany)">offset</a> produced at the top of the fruit<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PWG_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PWG-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> or from a side shoot, and typically matures within a year.<sup id="cite_ref-PWG_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PWG-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Pineapple flowers close-up</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The pineapple is a <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous_plant" title="Herbaceous plant">herbaceous</a> <a href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a>, which grows to 1.0 to 1.5&#160;m (3&#160;ft 3&#160;in to 4&#160;ft 11&#160;in) tall on average, although sometimes it can be taller. The plant has a short, stocky stem with tough, waxy leaves. When creating its fruit, it usually produces up to 200 flowers, although some large-fruited <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> can exceed this. Once it flowers, the individual fruits of the flowers join together to create a <a href="/wiki/Multiple_fruit" title="Multiple fruit">multiple fruit</a>. After the first fruit is produced, side shoots (called 'suckers' by commercial growers) are produced in the leaf axils of the main stem. These suckers may be removed for propagation, or left to produce additional fruits on the original plant.<sup id="cite_ref-PWG_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PWG-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Commercially, suckers that appear around the base are cultivated. It has 30 or more narrow, fleshy, trough-shaped leaves that are <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>30 to 100&#160;cm (1 to <span class="frac">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>&#160;ft) long, surrounding a thick <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">stem</a>; the leaves have sharp spines along the margins. In the first year of growth, the axis lengthens and thickens, bearing numerous leaves in close spirals. After 12 to 20 months, the stem grows into a spike-like <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> up to 15&#160;cm (6&#160;in) long with over 100 spirally arranged, trimerous flowers, each subtended by a bract.
</p><p>In the wild, pineapples are pollinated primarily by <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Certain wild pineapples are <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">foraged</a> and pollinated at night by <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Under cultivation, because seed development diminishes fruit quality, pollination is performed by hand, and seeds are retained only for <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">breeding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, where pineapples were cultivated and <a href="/wiki/Cannery" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannery">canned</a> industrially throughout the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-hortsci_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hortsci-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> importation of hummingbirds was prohibited.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ovaries develop into <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berries</a>, which coalesce into a large, compact, multiple fruit. The fruit of a pineapple is usually arranged in two interlocking <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helices</a>, often with 8 in one direction and 13 in the other, each being a <a href="/wiki/Fibonacci_number#Nature" class="mw-redirect" title="Fibonacci number">Fibonacci number</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pineapple carries out <a href="/wiki/Crassulacean_acid_metabolism" title="Crassulacean acid metabolism">CAM photosynthesis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> fixing carbon dioxide at night and storing it as the acid <a href="/wiki/Malate" class="mw-redirect" title="Malate">malate</a>, then releasing it during the day aiding photosynthesis.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<p>The pineapple comprises five botanical varieties, formerly regarded as separate species.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The genomes of three varieties, including the wild progenitor variety <i>bracteatus</i>, have been sequenced.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The first reference in English to the pineapple fruit was the 1568 translation from the French of <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Thevet" title="André Thevet">André Thevet</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Found_World,_or_Antarctike" title="The New Found World, or Antarctike">The New Found World, or Antarctike</a></i> where he refers to a <span title="Tupi languages collective text"><i lang="tup">Hoyriri</i></span>, a fruit cultivated and eaten by the <a href="/wiki/Tupinamb%C3%A1_people" title="Tupinambá people">Tupinambá people</a>, living near modern <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, and now believed to be a pineapple.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Later in the same English translation, he describes the same fruit as a "Nana made in the manner of a Pine apple", where he used another <a href="/wiki/Tupian_languages" title="Tupian languages">Tupi</a> word <span title="Tupi languages collective text"><i lang="tup">nanas</i></span>, meaning 'excellent fruit'.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This usage was adopted by many European languages and led to the plant's scientific <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Ananas comosus</i></span>, where <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">comosus</i></span> 'tufted' refers to the stem of the plant. Purchas, writing in English in 1613, referred to the fruit as <i>Ananas</i>, but the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> first record of the word <i>pineapple</i> itself by an English writer is by Mandeville in 1714.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg/220px-Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="185" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg/330px-Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg/440px-Pineapple_and_cross_section.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="1488" /></a><figcaption>Pineapple fruit, whole and in longitudinal section</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precolonial_cultivation">Precolonial cultivation</span></h3>
<p>The wild plant originates from the <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_River" title="Paraná River">Paraná</a>–<a href="/wiki/Paraguay_River" title="Paraguay River">Paraguay River</a> drainages between southern <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Little is known about its domestication, but it spread as a crop throughout South America. Archaeological evidence of use is found as far back as 1200–800 BC (3200–2800 BP) in Peru<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and 200 BC – 700 AD (2200–1300 BP) in Mexico,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> where it was cultivated by the <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Mayas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> By the late 1400s, cropped pineapple was widely distributed and a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> of Native Americans. The first European to encounter the pineapple was Columbus, in <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a> on 4 November 1493.<sup id="cite_ref-history_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The Portuguese took the fruit from Brazil and introduced it into <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> by 1550.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The '<a href="/w/index.php?title=Red_Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Red Spanish (page does not exist)">Red Spanish</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi%C3%B1a_Espa%C3%B1ola_Roja" class="extiw" title="es:Piña Española Roja">es</a>&#93;</span>' cultivar was also introduced by the Spanish from Latin America to the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and it was grown for textile use from at least the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-pfmf_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pfmf-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Columbus brought the plant back to Spain and called it <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">piña de Indes</i></span>, meaning "pine of the Indians". The pineapple was documented in Peter Martyr's <i><a href="/wiki/Decades_of_the_New_World" title="Decades of the New World">Decades of the New World</a></i> (1516) and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Pigafetta" title="Antonio Pigafetta">Antonio Pigafetta</a>'s <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Relazione del primo viaggio intorno al mondo</i></span> (1524–1525), and the first known illustration was in <a href="/wiki/Gonzalo_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Oviedo_y_Vald%C3%A9s" title="Gonzalo Fernández de Oviedo y Valdés">Oviedo's</a> <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Historia General de Las Indias</i></span> (1535).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_World_introduction">Old World introduction</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="313" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-The_gardeners_dictionary_Wellcome_L0049859_frontispiece_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3161" data-file-height="4495" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a> presented with <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Cornucopia" title="Cornucopia">cornucopiae</a></i></span> including pineapples by allegories of Nature, Industry, and Science, with an <a href="/wiki/Orangery" title="Orangery">orangery</a> in the background (<a href="/wiki/Book_frontispiece" title="Book frontispiece">frontispiece</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gardeners_Dictionary" title="The Gardeners Dictionary">The Gardeners Dictionary</a></i>, 1764)</figcaption></figure>
<p>While the pineapple fascinated Europeans as a fruit of colonialism,<sup id="cite_ref-Cumo_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cumo-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> it was not successfully cultivated in Europe until <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pieter_de_la_Court_van_der_Voort&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pieter de la Court van der Voort (page does not exist)">Pieter de la Court</a> (1664–1739) developed <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouse</a> horticulture near Leiden.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Pineapple plants were distributed from the Netherlands to English gardeners in 1719 and French ones in 1730.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In England, the first pineapple was grown at <a href="/wiki/Dorney_Court" title="Dorney Court">Dorney Court</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorney" title="Dorney">Dorney</a> in Buckinghamshire, and a huge "pineapple stove" to heat the plants was built at the <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Physic_Garden" title="Chelsea Physic Garden">Chelsea Physic Garden</a> in 1723.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sheller_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheller-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In France, King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XV" title="Louis XV">Louis XV</a> was presented with a pineapple that had been grown at <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Versailles" class="mw-redirect" title="Garden of Versailles">Versailles</a> in 1733. In Russia, <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> imported de la Court's method into <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a> in the 1720s; in 1730, twenty pineapple saplings were transported from there to a greenhouse at <a href="/wiki/Anna_of_Russia" title="Anna of Russia">Empress Anna</a>'s new Moscow palace.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ananas_fructu_ovato,_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus,_foliis_Aloes._(Pineapple)_(NYPL_b14444147-1125000).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ananas_fructu_ovato%2C_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus%2C_foliis_Aloes._%28Pineapple%29_%28NYPL_b14444147-1125000%29.jpg/220px-Ananas_fructu_ovato%2C_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus%2C_foliis_Aloes._%28Pineapple%29_%28NYPL_b14444147-1125000%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="350" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ananas_fructu_ovato%2C_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus%2C_foliis_Aloes._%28Pineapple%29_%28NYPL_b14444147-1125000%29.jpg/330px-Ananas_fructu_ovato%2C_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus%2C_foliis_Aloes._%28Pineapple%29_%28NYPL_b14444147-1125000%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ananas_fructu_ovato%2C_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus%2C_foliis_Aloes._%28Pineapple%29_%28NYPL_b14444147-1125000%29.jpg/440px-Ananas_fructu_ovato%2C_carne_albida_-_Carduus_Brasilianus%2C_foliis_Aloes._%28Pineapple%29_%28NYPL_b14444147-1125000%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3086" data-file-height="4906" /></a><figcaption>1772 illustration of an <i>Ananas comosus</i> pineapple which was given the early scientific name of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Cardus brasilianus folius aloes</i></span> by Banhius in 1623<sup id="cite_ref-Bromeliad_Society_Chicago_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bromeliad_Society_Chicago-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Because of the expense of direct import and the enormous cost in equipment and labour required to grow them in a temperate climate, in greenhouses called "pineries", <a href="/wiki/Pineapple_mania" title="Pineapple mania">pineapple became a symbol of wealth</a>. They were initially used mainly for display at dinner parties, rather than being eaten, and were used again and again until they began to rot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeauman200587_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeauman200587-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In the second half of the 18th century, the production of the fruit on British estates became the subject of great rivalry between wealthy aristocrats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeauman200587_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeauman200587-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Murray,_4th_Earl_of_Dunmore" title="John Murray, 4th Earl of Dunmore">John Murray, 4th Earl of Dunmore</a>, built a hothouse on his estate surmounted by a huge stone <a href="/wiki/Cupola" title="Cupola">cupola</a> 14 metres tall in the shape of the fruit; it is known as the <a href="/wiki/Dunmore_Pineapple" title="Dunmore Pineapple">Dunmore Pineapple</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In architecture, pineapple figures became decorative elements symbolizing hospitality.<sup id="cite_ref-Curl_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curl-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morrison_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morrison-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harris_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_the_19th_century:_mass_commercialization">Since the 19th century: mass commercialization</span></h3>
<p>Many different varieties, mostly from the Antilles, were tried for European glasshouse cultivation. The most significant was "Smooth Cayenne", imported to France in 1820, subsequently re-exported to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> in 1835, and then from the UK via <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> to Australia and Africa. "Smooth Cayenne" is now the dominant cultivar in world production.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Jams and sweets based on pineapple were imported to Europe from the West Indies, Brazil, and Mexico from an early date. By the early 19th century, fresh pineapples were transported direct from the West Indies in large enough quantities to reduce European prices.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Later pineapple production was dominated by the Azores for Europe, and Florida and the Caribbean for North America, because of the short trade routes.
</p><p>The Spanish had introduced the pineapple into Hawaii in the 18th century<sup id="cite_ref-MatthewsWigsten2010_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MatthewsWigsten2010-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> where it is known as the <span title="Hawaiian-language text"><i lang="haw">hala kahiki</i></span> ("foreign <a href="/wiki/Pandanus_tectorius" title="Pandanus tectorius">hala</a>"),<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> but the first commercial plantation was established in 1886. The most famous investor was <a href="/wiki/James_Dole" title="James Dole">James Dole</a>, who moved to Hawaii in 1899<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and started a 24-hectare (60-acre) pineapple plantation in 1900 which would grow into the <a href="/wiki/Dole_Food_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Dole Food Company">Dole Food Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-faculty.ucc.edu_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faculty.ucc.edu-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Dole and <a href="/wiki/Del_Monte_Foods" title="Del Monte Foods">Del Monte</a> began growing pineapples on the island of <a href="/wiki/Oahu" title="Oahu">Oahu</a> in 1901 and 1917, respectively, and the <a href="/wiki/Maui_Pineapple_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Maui Pineapple Company">Maui Pineapple Company</a> began cultivation on <a href="/wiki/Maui" title="Maui">Maui</a> in 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> James Dole began the commercial processing of pineapple, and Dole employee Henry Ginaca invented an automatic peeling and coring machine in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_25-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Drummond_Dole.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/James_Drummond_Dole.jpg/220px-James_Drummond_Dole.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="302" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/James_Drummond_Dole.jpg/330px-James_Drummond_Dole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/James_Drummond_Dole.jpg/440px-James_Drummond_Dole.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6072" data-file-height="8344" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/James_Dole" title="James Dole">James Drummond Dole</a> (1877–1958) was the early promoter of the pineapple industry in Hawaii. He founded the company now known as the Dole Food Company.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips,_Bukidnon,_Philippines_03.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips%2C_Bukidnon%2C_Philippines_03.jpg/220px-Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips%2C_Bukidnon%2C_Philippines_03.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips%2C_Bukidnon%2C_Philippines_03.jpg/330px-Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips%2C_Bukidnon%2C_Philippines_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips%2C_Bukidnon%2C_Philippines_03.jpg/440px-Del_Monte_Pineapple_field_at_Camp_Philips%2C_Bukidnon%2C_Philippines_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="2268" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Del_Monte_Foods" title="Del Monte Foods">Del Monte</a> pineapple fields in <a href="/wiki/Bukidnon" title="Bukidnon">Bukidnon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Hawaiian production started to decline from the 1970s because of competition and the shift to refrigerated sea transport. Dole ceased its cannery operations in <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a> in 1991, and in 2008, Del Monte terminated its pineapple-growing operations in Hawaii.<sup id="cite_ref-rhodes_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rhodes-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, the Maui Pineapple Company reduced its operations to supply pineapples only locally on Maui,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and by 2013, only the <a href="/wiki/Dole_Plantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Dole Plantation">Dole Plantation</a> on Oahu grew pineapples in a volume of about 0.1 percent of the world's production.<sup id="cite_ref-rhodes_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rhodes-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Despite this decline, the pineapple is sometimes used as a symbol of Hawaii.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Further, foods with pineapple in them are sometimes known as "Hawaiian" for this reason alone.
</p><p>In the Philippines, "Smooth Cayenne" was introduced in the early 1900s by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">US Bureau of Agriculture</a> during the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines_(1898%E2%80%931946)" title="History of the Philippines (1898–1946)">American colonial period</a>. Dole and Del Monte established plantations in the island of <a href="/wiki/Mindanao" title="Mindanao">Mindanao</a> in the 1920s; in the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Cotabato" title="Cotabato">Cotabato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bukidnon" title="Bukidnon">Bukidnon</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-pfmf_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pfmf-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-go_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-go-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Large scale canning had started in Southeast Asia, including in the Philippines, from 1920. This trade was severely damaged by <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and Hawaii dominated the international trade until the 1960s.
</p><p>The Philippines remain one of the top exporters of pineapples in the world. The Del Monte plantations are now locally managed, after Del Monte Pacific Ltd., a Filipino company, completed the purchase of Del Monte Foods in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1218072481">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data div{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data div{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}</style><table class="infobox nowrap"><caption class="infobox-title" style="white-space:normal; <p>Raw pineapple pulp is 86% water, 13% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 0.5% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table). In a 100-gram reference amount, raw pineapple supplies 209 kilojoules (50 kilocalories) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>, and is a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (44% <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Daily Value</a>, DV) and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> (58% DV), but otherwise contains no <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant amounts.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg/220px-Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg/330px-Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg/440px-Hollowed_Pineapple_Core_Removed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a><figcaption>A hollowed-out pineapple with its core left intact, ready for filling, eg with other fruits.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemistry">Phytochemistry</span></h3>
<p>Pineapple fruits and peels contain diverse <a href="/wiki/Phytochemical" title="Phytochemical">phytochemicals</a>, among which are <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Gallic_acid" title="Gallic acid">gallic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syringic_acid" title="Syringic acid">syringic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanillin" title="Vanillin">vanillin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferulic_acid" title="Ferulic acid">ferulic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinapic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinapic acid">sinapic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coumaric_acid" title="Coumaric acid">coumaric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorogenic_acid" title="Chlorogenic acid">chlorogenic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epicatechin" class="mw-redirect" title="Epicatechin">epicatechin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arbutin" title="Arbutin">arbutin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Present in all parts of the pineapple plant,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bromelain" title="Bromelain">bromelain</a> is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Proteolytic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteolytic">proteolytic</a> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. It is present in stem, fruit, crown, core, leaves of pineapple itself.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Bromelain is under preliminary research for treatment of a variety of clinical disorders, but has not been adequately defined for its effects in the human body.<sup id="cite_ref-mlp_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlp-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Bromelain may be unsafe for some users, such as in <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Allergies">allergies</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Anticoagulation">anticoagulation</a> therapy.<sup id="cite_ref-mlp_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlp-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Having sufficient bromelain content, raw pineapple juice may be useful as a meat <a href="/wiki/Marinade" class="mw-redirect" title="Marinade">marinade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tenderizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenderizer">tenderizer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jfst_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfst-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Although pineapple enzymes can interfere with the preparation of some foods or manufactured products, such as <a href="/wiki/Gelatin" title="Gelatin">gelatin</a>-based desserts or <a href="/wiki/Gel" title="Gel">gel</a> <a href="/wiki/Capsule_(pharmacy)" title="Capsule (pharmacy)">capsules</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> their proteolytic activity responsible for such properties may be degraded during cooking and <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canning</a>. The quantity of bromelain in a typical serving of pineapple fruit is probably not significant, but specific <a href="/wiki/Extraction_(chemistry)" title="Extraction (chemistry)">extraction</a> can yield sufficient quantities for <a href="/wiki/Home" title="Home">domestic</a> and industrial processing.<sup id="cite_ref-jfst_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfst-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivars">Cultivars</span></h3>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> are known.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The leaves of the commonly grown "smooth cayenne" are smooth,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and it is the most commonly grown worldwide. Many cultivars have become distributed from its origins in <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> and the southern part of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, and later improved stocks were introduced into the Americas, the Azores, Africa, India, Malaysia and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Varieties include:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"Hilo" is a compact, 1.0- to 1.5-kg (2– to 3-lb) Hawaiian variant of smooth cayenne; the fruit is more cylindrical and produces many suckers, but no slips.</li>
<li>"Kona sugarloaf", at 2.5 to 3.0&#160;kg (5–6&#160;lb), has white flesh with no woodiness in the center, is cylindrical in shape, and has a high sugar content but no acid; it has an unusually sweet fruit.</li>
<li>"Natal queen", at 1.0 to 1.5&#160;kg (2 to 3&#160;lb), has golden yellow flesh, crisp texture, and delicate mild flavor; well-adapted to fresh consumption, it keeps well after ripening. It has spiny leaves and is grown in Australia, Malaysia, and South Africa.</li>
<li>"Pernambuco" ("eleuthera") weighs 1–2&#160;kg (2–4&#160;lb), and has pale yellow to white flesh. It is sweet, melting in texture, and excellent for eating fresh; it is poorly adapted for shipping, has spiny leaves, and is grown in Latin America.</li>
<li>"Red Spanish", at 1–2&#160;kg (2–4&#160;lb), has pale yellow flesh with a pleasant aroma, is squarish in shape, and well-adapted for shipping as fresh fruit to distant markets; it has spiny leaves and is grown in Latin America and the Philippines. It was the original pineapple cultivar in the Philippines grown for their leaf fibers (<i><a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%B1a" title="Piña">piña</a></i>) in the traditional Philippine textile industry.<sup id="cite_ref-pfmf_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pfmf-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-go_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-go-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>"Smooth cayenne", a 2.5- to 3.0-kg (5- to 6-lb), pale yellow– to yellow-fleshed, cylindrical fruit with high sugar and acid content, is well-adapted to canning and processing; its leaves are without spines. It is an ancient cultivar developed by <a href="/wiki/Amerind_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Amerind peoples">Amerind peoples</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartholomew_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartholomew-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In some parts of Asia, this cultivar is known as <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>, after an area of Malaysia in which it is grown.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> It is one of the ancestors of cultivars "73-50" (also called "MD-1" and "CO-2") and "73–114" (also called "MD-2").<sup id="cite_ref-Bartholomew_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartholomew-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Smooth cayenne was previously the variety produced in Hawaii, and the most easily obtainable in U.S. grocery stores, but was replaced over the course of the mid-1990s and 2000s by MD-2.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartholomew_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartholomew-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Some <i>Ananas</i> species are grown as <a href="/wiki/Ornamental_plant" title="Ornamental plant">ornamentals</a> for color, novel fruit size, and other <a href="/wiki/Aesthetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aesthetic">aesthetic</a> qualities.</li></ul>
<p>In the US, in 1986, the Pineapple Research Institute was dissolved and its assets divided between Del Monte and <a href="/wiki/Maui_Land_and_Pineapple" class="mw-redirect" title="Maui Land and Pineapple">Maui Land and Pineapple</a>. Del Monte took cultivar '73–114', dubbed 'MD-2', to its plantations in Costa Rica, found it to be well-suited to growing there, and launched it publicly in 1996 as 'Gold Extra Sweet', while Del Monte also began marketing '73–50', dubbed 'CO-2', as 'Del Monte Gold'.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartholomew_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartholomew-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Maui Pineapple Company began growing variety 73-50 in 1988 and named it Maui Gold.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The successor company to MPC, the Hali'imaile Pineapple Company continues to grow Maui Gold on the slopes of <a href="/wiki/Haleakal%C4%81" title="Haleakalā">Haleakala</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_pineapple_production" title="List of countries by pineapple production">List of countries by pineapple production</a></div>
<p>In 2022, world production of pineapples was 29 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, each producing about 3 million tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-faostat_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary">Culinary</span></h3>
<p>The flesh and juice of the pineapple are used in cuisines around the world. In many tropical countries, pineapple is prepared and sold on roadsides as a snack. It is sold whole or in halves with a stick inserted. Whole, cored slices with a <a href="/wiki/Cherry" title="Cherry">cherry</a> in the middle are a common garnish on hams in the West. Chunks of pineapple are used in desserts such as fruit salad, as well as in some savory dishes, including the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_pizza" title="Hawaiian pizza">Hawaiian pizza</a>, or as a grilled ring on a <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburger</a>. Traditional dishes that use pineapple include <span title="Tagalog-language text"><i lang="tl"><a href="/wiki/Hamonado" title="Hamonado">hamonado</a></i></span>, <span title="Tagalog-language text"><i lang="tl"><a href="/wiki/Afritada" title="Afritada">afritada</a></i></span>, <span title="Thai-language text"><i lang="th"><a href="/wiki/Kaeng_som" title="Kaeng som">kaeng som pla</a></i></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_haystack" title="Hawaiian haystack">Hawaiian haystack</a>. Crushed pineapple is used in yogurt, jam, sweets, and ice cream. The juice of the pineapple is served as a beverage, and it is also the main ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Cocktail" title="Cocktail">cocktails</a> such as the <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%B1a_colada" title="Piña colada">piña colada</a></i></span> and in the drink <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Tepache" title="Tepache">tepache</a></i></span>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, a traditional jelly-like dessert called <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Nata_de_pi%C3%B1a" title="Nata de piña">nata de piña</a></i></span> has also been produced since the 18th century. It is made by fermenting pineapple juice with the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Komagataeibacter_xylinus" title="Komagataeibacter xylinus">Komagataeibacter xylinus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vergara_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vergara-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pineapple vinegar is an ingredient found in both <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Honduras" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuisine of Honduras">Honduran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Filipino_cuisine" title="Filipino cuisine">Filipino cuisine</a>, where it is produced locally.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">Mexico</a>, it is usually made with peels from the whole fruit, rather than the juice; however, in <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_cuisine" title="Taiwanese cuisine">Taiwanese cuisine</a>, it is often produced by blending pineapple juice with grain vinegar.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> consumed 50% of the global total for pineapple juice in 2012–2016. The <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> was the largest importer of pineapple juice in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> are the major suppliers to the European Union market in 2012–2016.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Countries consuming the most pineapple juice in 2017 were <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, having combined consumption of 47% of the world total. The consumption of pineapple juice in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is low compared to their populations.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textiles">Textiles</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%B1a" title="Piña">Piña</a></div>
<p>The 'Red Spanish' cultivar of pineapples were once extensively cultivated in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. The long leaves of the cultivar were the source of traditional <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%B1a" title="Piña">piña</a></i></span> fibers, an adaptation of the native weaving traditions with fibers extracted from <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Abac%C3%A1" title="Abacá">abacá</a></i></span>. These were woven into lustrous lace-like <span title="Tagalog-language text"><i lang="tl"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nipis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nipis (page does not exist)">nipis</a></i></span> fabrics usually decorated with intricate floral embroidery known as <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">calado</i></span> and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">sombrado</i></span>. The fabric was a luxury export from the Philippines during the Spanish colonial period and gained favor among European aristocracy in the 18th and 19th centuries. Domestically, they were used to make the traditional <span title="Tagalog-language text"><i lang="tl"><a href="/wiki/Barong_tagalog" title="Barong tagalog">barong tagalog</a></i></span>, <span title="Tagalog-language text"><i lang="tl"><a href="/wiki/Baro%27t_saya" title="Baro&#39;t saya">baro't saya</a></i></span>, and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Traje_de_mestiza" class="mw-redirect" title="Traje de mestiza">traje de mestiza</a></i></span> clothing of the Filipino upper class, as well as women's <a href="/wiki/Kerchief" title="Kerchief">kerchiefs</a> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">pañuelo</i></span>). They were favored for their light and breezy quality, which was ideal in the hot tropical climate of the islands. The industry was destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> and is only starting to be revived.<sup id="cite_ref-pfmf_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pfmf-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-go_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-go-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Houseplant">Houseplant</span></h3>
<p>The variety <i>A. comosus</i> 'Variegatus' is occasionally grown as a houseplant. It needs direct sunlight and thrives at temperatures of 18 to 24&#160;°C (64 to 75&#160;°F), with a minimum winter temperature of 16&#160;°C (61&#160;°F). It should be kept humid, but the soil should be allowed to dry out between waterings. It has almost no resting period but should be repotted each spring until the container reaches 20 centimeters (8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<p>In commercial farming, flowering can be induced artificially, and the early harvesting of the main fruit can encourage the development of a second crop of smaller fruits. Once removed during cleaning, the top of the pineapple can be planted in soil and a new plant will grow. Slips and suckers are planted commercially.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage_and_transport">Storage and transport</span></h3>
<p>Some buyers prefer green fruit, others ripened or off-green. A plant growth regulator, <a href="/wiki/Ethephon" title="Ethephon">Ethephon</a>, is typically sprayed onto the fruit one week before harvest, developing <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_as_a_plant_hormone#ethylene" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethylene as a plant hormone">ethylene</a>, which turns the fruit golden yellow. After cleaning and slicing, a pineapple is typically canned in sugar syrup with added preservative.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A pineapple <a href="/wiki/Climacteric_(botany)" title="Climacteric (botany)">never becomes any riper than it was when harvested</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethical_and_environmental_concerns">Ethical and environmental concerns</span></h3>
<p>Like most <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">modern fruit production</a>, pineapple plantations are highly industrialized operations. In Costa Rica particularly, the pineapple industry uses large amounts of insecticides to protect the crop, which have caused health problems in many workers. These workers often receive little compensation, and are mostly poor migrants, often <a href="/wiki/Nicaraguan" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicaraguan">Nicaraguan</a>. Workers' wages also decrease every time prices are lowered overseas. In 2016, the government declared that it would be trying to improve the situation, with the help of various other groups.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, tropical fruit agriculture, such as for pineapples, has been concentrated in so-called "<a href="/wiki/Banana_republics" class="mw-redirect" title="Banana republics">banana republics</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Illegal_drug_trade">Illegal drug trade</span></h4>
<p>Export pineapples from Costa Rica to Europe are often used as a cover for <a href="/wiki/Illegal_drug_trade" title="Illegal drug trade">narcotrafficking</a>, and containers are impounded routinely in both locations.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_into_protected_areas">Expansion into protected areas</span></h4>
<p>In Costa Rica, pineapple cultivation has expanded into the <a href="/wiki/Maquenque_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Maquenque National Wildlife Refuge">Maquenque</a>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Border_Corridor_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Border Corridor Wildlife Refuge">Corredor Fronterizo</a></i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Barra_del_Colorado_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Barra del Colorado Wildlife Refuge">Barra del Colorado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1o_Negro_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Caño Negro Wildlife Refuge">Caño Negro</a> wildlife refuges, all located in the north of the country. As those are protected areas and not national parks, limited and restricted sustainable activities are allowed, however pineapple plantations are industrial operations and many of these do not have the proper license to operate in the protected areas, or were started before either the designation of the area, recent regulations or the creation of the environmental regulatory agency (Setena) in 1996. The agency has registers for around 358.5&#160;ha (1.384&#160;sq&#160;mi) of pineapple plantations operating within protected areas, but satellite imagery from 2018 reports around 1,659&#160;ha (6.41&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_pineapple_diseases" title="List of pineapple diseases">List of pineapple diseases</a></div>
<p>Pineapples are subject to a variety of diseases, the most serious of which is wilt disease vectored by <a href="/wiki/Mealybug" title="Mealybug">mealybugs</a><sup id="cite_ref-aps_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aps-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> typically found on the surface of pineapples, but possibly in the closed blossom cups.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Other diseases include citrus <a href="/wiki/Phanerochaete_salmonicolor" class="mw-redirect" title="Phanerochaete salmonicolor">pink disease</a>, bacterial heart rot, <a href="/wiki/Anthracnose" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthracnose">anthracnose</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-aps_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aps-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> fungal heart rot, root rot, black rot, butt rot, fruitlet core rot, and yellow spot virus.<sup id="cite_ref-foodmarketexchange1_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foodmarketexchange1-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Pineapple pink disease (not citrus pink disease) is characterized by the fruit developing a brownish to black discoloration when heated during the canning process. The causal agents of pink disease are the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Acetobacter_aceti" title="Acetobacter aceti">Acetobacter aceti</a></i>, <i>Gluconobacter oxydans</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pantoea" title="Pantoea">Pantoea</a> citrea</i><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Tatumella ptyseos</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some pests that commonly affect pineapple plants are scales, <a href="/wiki/Thrip" class="mw-redirect" title="Thrip">thrips</a>, mites, mealybugs, ants, and <a href="/wiki/Symphylid" class="mw-redirect" title="Symphylid">symphylids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-foodmarketexchange1_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foodmarketexchange1-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Heart-rot is the most serious disease affecting pineapple plants. The disease is caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_cinnamomi" title="Phytophthora cinnamomi">Phytophthora cinnamomi</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_parasitica" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytophthora parasitica">P. parasitica</a></i>, fungi that often affect pineapples grown in wet conditions. Since it is difficult to treat, it is advisable to guard against infection by planting resistant cultivars where these are available; all <a href="/wiki/Suckers_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Suckers (botany)">suckers</a> that are required for propagation should be dipped in a fungicide, since the fungus enters through the wounds.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
